201512	1045	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Thank you, Chair Yellen.	13	1
201512	1046	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	To start, let me go back and go a little deeper on the energy sector.	13	1
201512	1047	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	A number have commented on it.	13	1
201512	1048	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Obviously, the overall tone has soured since the previous FOMC meeting.	13	1
201512	1049	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The attitude has now shifted to an even-lower-for-even- longer price outlook.	13	1
201512	1050	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We have all talked about the disarray in OPEC and the lack of any pretense of a production ceiling.	13	1
201512	1051	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Market participants that we talked to in the Eleventh District believe that Saudi Arabia is quite content with the results so far of their supply strategy.	13	1
201512	1052	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	They are determined to maintain their market share, and certainly any reduction in supply from Saudi Arabia is going to have to be accompanied by proportionate declines from Russia and Middle Eastern nations, and that is really not feasible.	13	1
201512	1053	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So a reduction appears to be unlikely so far.	13	1
201512	1054	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We think that OECD excess inventory, which we measure as the amount of inventory that is over the five-year average, is now about 300 million barrels globally.	13	1
201512	1055	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I have talked in the past two meetings about the daily supply–demand gap.	13	1
201512	1056	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	By the time we end this year, we think the year will have averaged a daily supply–demand gap of about 1.7 million barrels.	13	1
201512	1057	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I think when I first started talking in September we said 2.	13	1
201512	1058	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	At our previous meeting we said something lower, 1.6.	13	1
201512	1059	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We now believe—actually, today—that the supply–demand gap is 1.2 million barrels.	13	1
201512	1060	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We expect that, by the end of 2016, that gap—it won’t be linear, but it will get down to 600,000 barrels a day.	13	1
201512	1061	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And based on all that, we continue to believe that it’s probably not going to be until—as we’ve said before—if not late 2016, then sometime in the first half of 2017 before daily supply–demand gets into some amount of balance and we start working off this inventory.	13	1
201512	1062	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	If we are wrong, though, we are going to be wrong because it is going to take longer, and here is why and here is what is spooking the market.	13	1
201512	1063	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It starts with Iran.	13	1
201512	1064	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I have always mentioned that Iran was a wild card.	13	1
201512	1065	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And because it now looks much more probable that sanctions in Iran are going to be lifted, and lifted sooner than the market had expected, we now believe Iran will likely return to the market at some point in the first half of 2016.	13	1
201512	1066	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Today, they produce 2.8 million barrels.	13	1
201512	1067	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We’ve talked to market participants broadly, and we’re not sure exactly what the Iranians do with that 2.8 million barrels, but the suspicion is that they’re selling some of it, and they’re storing some of it.	13	1
201512	1068	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We know that it would be their intention to increase their supply to 3.3 million barrels a day.	13	1
201512	1069	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We also know that they currently have in storage 30 million to 40 million barrels of oil.	13	1
201512	1070	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So when you talk about those supply–demand numbers that I said earlier, they don’t factor in Iran.	13	1
201512	1071	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	But these are very big numbers, and it has thrown the market into a great amount of disarray, and it has created more uncertainty about when the balance is going to happen.	13	1
201512	1072	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We have also mentioned Libya, but it is much smaller.	13	1
201512	1073	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	That will add another 200,000 to 400,000 barrels of oil, but that is small compared with Iran.	13	1
201512	1074	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Another thing we have learned—and this has been interesting about the structure of this industry—we’ve said a lot about all of the rig count cuts and the cap-ex cuts.	13	1
201512	1075	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	But the truth is that US production declines have been very slow to materialize, and global production declines have been slow.	13	1
201512	1076	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	One of the reasons is that there have been production increases from the Gulf of Mexico, which was a very long-term project that just got activated.	13	1
201512	1077	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	That went the other way.	13	1
201512	1078	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Also, there was a bump-up in oil prices at the end of the summer, which caused some shale producers who are very nimble to reinitiate production and sell in the forward curve.	13	1
201512	1079	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So that created a problem.	13	1
201512	1080	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	There is also a time lag between rig and spending declines and actual production declines.	13	1
201512	1081	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Normally, producers don’t just shut down a well.	13	1
201512	1082	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	They let it run off, and they simply don’t replace it with new supply.	13	1
201512	1083	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Also, we’re now finding that these rigs in shale are much more productive.	13	1
201512	1084	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	This is one place in which we are seeing productivity growth, for better or worse, and they’re producing more from wells than they may have expected.	13	1
201512	1085	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So here’s what we think is happening.	13	1
201512	1086	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Because global supply has been sticky to decline, there is even more pressure domestically to decrease— and we’re all seeing it.	13	1
201512	1087	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	What’s happening here is that there is much more pressure on domestic producers and their service suppliers, so there’s going to be a further decline.	13	1
201512	1088	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It’s going to happen in a step function.	13	1
201512	1089	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	One of the reasons we are getting more into balance, though, is demand.	13	1
201512	1090	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Our estimate is that demand growth in 2015 was 1.5 million barrels a day, so that helped create some of it.	13	1
201512	1091	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And we think it is going to be another 1.5 million barrels a day in 2016.	13	1
201512	1092	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So that alone will help get us into balance.	13	1
201512	1093	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It is ironic to talk about this now, but there is more and more discussion—and it is starting to make sense to me—that by 2018, we may actually get to the point at which we’re in balance, having worked off a lot of this excess inventory.	13	1
201512	1094	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We will see what decisions OPEC makes then, and there is going to be a lag to get supply back online in 2018, because a lot of the service providers and the equipment and the labor will have been taken out of commission, and people are going to be leery about getting back in.	13	1
201512	1095	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The irony is that we might get whipsawed, or we might have a price shock in 2018—probably not before then, but in 2018 and beyond there is going to be some risk of this.	13	1
201512	1096	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I have a feeling we’re going to be talking about this at this table because of the damage that is being done here to domestic supply, and that OPEC might, at a future date, depending on what happens, reassert itself.	13	1
201512	1097	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So we will talk about that more.	13	1
201512	1098	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Not surprisingly, more bankruptcies, restructurings, and so on and so forth—it is going to get worse in that regard.	13	1
201512	1099	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I’ll comment on the high-yield market, which we talked about earlier, because the energy sector is a material part of the high-yield market.	13	1
201512	1100	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	High-yield credit spreads have widened, and the dynamic is that, because energy is weak, there are more defaults or risk of defaults.	13	1
201512	1101	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	High- yield funds used to be held by insurance companies in the old days and, yes, by some funds and others, but an increasing amount of the holdings in the high-yield market are by people who offer daily liquidity or even monthly or quarterly liquidity.	13	1
201512	1102	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And what’s happening with weak energy is, you don’t sell the energy because you probably can’t sell it, but you will sell a double-B or other stronger credit, which widens high-yield spreads for everyone in the market.	13	1
201512	1103	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We are seeing that in the hospital sector this week—it’s a great example.	13	1
201512	1104	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The hospital sector really gapped out this week even though there’s been no credit event, but that’s a ripple effect arising from what’s going on in energy.	13	1
201512	1105	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We’re at risk of that happening again.	13	1
201512	1106	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	If Iran really does come back on, and we have more shocks and this gets worse, we may have more events like we did this last week heading into 2016.	13	1
201512	1107	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We are just going to have to monitor it.	13	1
201512	1108	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I’ll briefly say a couple of words about the District.	13	1
201512	1109	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Obviously, these facts—low oil prices, a slowing international economy, a strengthened dollar—are not helpful to the Eleventh District.	13	1
201512	1110	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	There has been strength in the health-care and the leisure-and-hospitality sectors.	13	1
201512	1111	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The Affordable Care Act has been helpful.	13	1
201512	1112	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Also, the drops in gasoline prices have certainly helped the leisure-and-hospitality industry in the Eleventh District.	13	1
201512	1113	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Similar to what we’ve said before, I’ll just summarize—from all of our surveys, the headline is: manufacturing weak, service sector remains strong.	13	1
201512	1114	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Because of the diversification of the state, we still think Texas’s job growth this year is likely to be 1.3 percent.	13	1
201512	1115	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We think job growth for Texas, for the District, will be 1.5 percent next year.	13	1
201512	1116	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	One of the key underpinnings of Texas’s growth for all of these years since 2000 is that we have had a higher rate of migration to and population growth in the state.	13	1
201512	1117	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Our population has grown 1 full percentage point faster than the rest of the country for the past 15 years on average.	13	1
201512	1118	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The impact of this will be, if jobs grow in Texas in 2016 at 1.5 percent, that our unemployment rate is likely to go up.	13	1
201512	1119	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It will inch up because this labor flow and company migration is continuing.	13	1
201512	1120	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It may stop or slow the decline in the unemployment rate because of this.	13	1
201512	1121	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The last comment I will make—which is not a very positive thing, but it is something we are watching—is that Texas leads the nation in uninsured.	13	1
201512	1122	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	We have 19 percent uninsured, and this is the greatest percentage and number of uninsured in the country.	13	1
201512	1123	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	I won’t get into the debates of both sides, but we are one of the states that has not taken government money related to the Affordable Care Act and Medicaid.	13	1
201512	1124	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Without making a value judgment on this, this is something we are watching to see how it affects economic conditions.	13	1
201512	1125	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Regarding the national economy, I’m going to confine my comments, given time, to three or four things that we are hearing from CEOs who are domiciled in this District but do business throughout the United States.	13	1
201512	1126	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	These are a few trends that struck me as we talked:  Number one, most of our companies across sectors—service and manufacturing—see the consumer as healthier but as being notably cautious despite the gasoline-price dividends.	13	1
201512	1127	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Spending habits are also rapidly changing, driven by technology and globalization.	13	1
201512	1128	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Consumers are increasingly able to shop for goods, as we all know, at the lowest price, substituting private-label and non-US sources for brands, increasingly using technology to find deals, and turning to disruptive models as alternatives to traditional models—famously Uber versus a car or taxi, Airbnb versus a hotel room, and Amazon versus a book store or retailer.	13	1
201512	1129	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	But we are hearing these comments across sectors, including from AT&T, which is seeing this affect their business.	13	1
201512	1130	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	The impact of all of this is that consumer pricing power is driving down the price of goods and services.	13	1
201512	1131	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	In their day jobs, they may not have wage and bargaining power, but as consumers, they have pricing power.	13	1
201512	1132	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Exceptions to this include prescription drugs, health and beauty aids, and so-called affordable luxury, like a Starbucks cup of coffee and other experiences.	13	1
201512	1133	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	As a result, though, most companies that we talk to are continuing to search for ways to cut costs through process improvements in either manufacturing or technology they use to serve customers.	13	1
201512	1134	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Related to this, then, is a continuing trend in the effort to improve profits and EPS growth by leveraging up, as I mentioned in the previous meeting, to buy back stock or looking to do M&A to gain efficiencies.	13	1
201512	1135	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	This is highly creative.	13	1
201512	1136	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	There is widespread pressure from activists to do this.	13	1
201512	1137	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	At a 2 percent plus 10-year and tight investment-grade spreads, it’s hard to pass this up.	13	1
201512	1138	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Most of the businesses we talked to believe even with the first stages of federal funds tightening, it is unlikely to affect their availability of financing if they are double-B and stronger, and so they’re going to continue to do this—and I agree with this.	13	1
201512	1139	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Related to this, they are continuing to be hesitant to commit resources to capital spending because they don’t see the demand strength, as well as this phenomenon of downward pricing pressure, which squeezes their margins and expected ROI on projects.	13	1
201512	1140	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	My last comment is that a number of people I talked to, big and small companies, are commenting about a reduction in dynamism they’re seeing in various industries.	13	1
201512	1141	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	What do they mean?	13	1
201512	1142	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	It is easier to exit than to enter.	13	1
201512	1143	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	There are greater barriers to entry, including compliance with regulation, and, notably, this includes local regulation, licensing requirements, and harder- to-access credit.	13	1
201512	1144	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And I think President Lacker talked about the challenges faced by the community banks.	13	1
201512	1145	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	This is seeping into the availability of credit if you are a small business, if you are a new entrant.	13	1
201512	1146	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Scale efficiencies are needed to increase profit.	13	1
201512	1147	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Geographic mobility of labor is tougher because of land prices—it is harder to move.	13	1
201512	1148	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	So it makes it more important to have access to multiple labor pools.	13	1
201512	1149	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	And so, simply, top-line growth is harder to generate, and margins are increasingly coming from scale.	13	1
201512	1150	2015-12-16	meeting	KAPLAN	Thank you.	13	1

201512	1156	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Since our previous meeting, the data have evolved largely as expected—in fact, even a little better—leaving little doubt about the strength and resilience of the expansion.	13	1
201512	1157	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Real GDP growth remains on track, bolstered by solid consumer and business spending.	13	1

201512	1165	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	For job growth over the past 12 months, 7 of our 9 states are ranked in the top 10, and this group includes states that were hit the hardest by the housing bust and the financial crisis, such as California, Nevada, and Arizona.	13	1

201512	1169	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	The size of that broad group has fallen dramatically over the past year and is now largely in line with levels observed in the past two expansions when the unemployment rate reached 5 percent.	13	1
201512	1170	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Other labor market indicators such as perceptions of job availability, job openings, and difficulty filling jobs are near or above their levels associated with full employment.	13	1
201512	1171	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Looking ahead, with above-trend GDP growth next year, my SEP submission has the labor market overshooting our full employment target by a substantial margin.	13	1

201512	1174	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Looking further ahead, I see growth needing to fall back to slightly below potential in order to get the unemployment rate back to its natural rate, which I see as happening in 2018.	13	1
201512	1175	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Therefore, in my SEP submission, I actually have in 2017 and 2018 growth of about 1¾ percent, which is below my estimate of trend.	13	1
201512	1176	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Now, this has nothing to do with the Phillips curve or anything—it’s simply the arithmetic of having an unemployment rate of 4½ percent and wanting it to move back to my natural rate of 5 percent in the next few years.	13	1
201512	1177	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	The sustained period of labor market overheating should increase upward pressure on inflation and offset some of the lingering disinflationary pressures associated with the pass- through of lower import and energy costs into core prices.	13	1

201512	1187	2015-12-16	meeting	WILLIAMS	Prices of apartment buildings in San Francisco and Silicon Valley have left their previous peaks in the dust and continue to soar.	13	1

201512	1203	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201512	1204	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	On a more serious note, my forecast has not changed a great deal since the September SEP submission.	13	1
201512	1205	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I see real GDP growth continuing modestly above the potential rate, although a bit slower in 2016 than the Tealbook growth forecast at 2½ percent, due to the headwinds arising from the external sector.	13	1
201512	1206	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The unemployment rate has declined six-tenths so far this year, and I continue to expect that it will decline to 4½ percent by the end of 2016 and remain near there in 2017 and 2018.	13	1
201512	1207	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I see core inflation moving up to 1½ percent next year and to around 2 percent in 2017, as the economy tightens and as the effects of a strong dollar and lower oil prices eventually fade.	13	1
201512	1208	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The two strong employment reports for October and November totaling a little more than 500,000 new jobs provide solid evidence to me that the economy is continuing to expand at an above-trend rate.	13	1
201512	1209	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Payrolls are now averaging comfortably above 200,000 for all of 2015 and for the past three months.	13	1
201512	1210	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Layoffs are low and job openings are high.	13	1
201512	1211	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I see continued growth supported by solid increases in consumer spending, reflecting a much-improved labor market, rising incomes, and an elevated wealth-to-income ratio.	13	1
201512	1212	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	In addition, residential investment and business investment outside the energy sector seem to be on a solid footing.	13	1
201512	1213	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Fiscal policy is now supporting the economy, rather than holding it back.	13	1
201512	1214	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The drag on aggregate demand due to the behavior of the external sector will continue, but that drag hasn’t been sufficient to slow the economy below trend.	13	1
201512	1215	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The amount of remaining slack is probably modest, but there is enough uncertainty about the location of supply-side constraints to counsel a cautious approach in removing accommodation.	13	1
201512	1216	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Without trying to address every margin of slack, I’d like to mention three areas in support of that.	13	1
201512	1217	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	First, while the unemployment rate of 5 percent is at or close to many estimates of the natural rate, I don’t yet see persuasive signs of an acceleration in compensation.	13	1
201512	1218	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The one indicator that does show a strong uptick is compensation per hour, and that is the highly volatile one, just as Governor Brainard said.	13	1
201512	1219	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	So I don’t see a sustainable or broad-based increase at all there, much as I stare at the graph.	13	1
201512	1220	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Such signs would be a welcome indicator of labor market tightness.	13	1
201512	1221	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Second, I would point to the labor force participation rate, which, at least to me, has been disappointing so far this year after remaining more or less flat for 18 months.	13	1
201512	1222	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	It’s now dropped four-tenths, but there is good reason to expect that participation will return to trend and probably exceed trend in the next couple of years on this path.	13	1
201512	1223	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The Tealbook estimate of the cyclical participation shortfall is about 30 basis points, consistent with the canonical paper by our staff—Stephanie Aaronson, Bill Wascher, and four others.	13	1
201512	1224	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	In the Tealbook forecast, participation moves sideways and in doing so reaches trend by around the end of 2016 and then actually moves slightly above trend thereafter, which is consistent with past recoveries.	13	1
201512	1225	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	In fact, in the past two recoveries, participation has risen to roughly 50 basis points above trend late in the cycle when wages are rising and the unemployment rate is low.	13	1
201512	1226	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I would also point to a good deal of interesting research that has documented the damage done to the labor force from long and deep recessions, and here I’m thinking of the work by Greg Howard, Rob Martin, and Beth Anne Wilson here at the Board, among others, for example.	13	1
201512	1227	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The bottom line is that I’m not in any hurry to conclude that the current low level of participation reflects immutable structural factors.	13	1
201512	1228	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Inflation provides a third reason for caution.	13	1
201512	1229	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	More recent movements in the dollar and oil should exert some downward pressure on prices for a while longer, although the staff estimates they would be modest.	13	1
201512	1230	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Although no one can predict the timing with any confidence, oil and the dollar will eventually stabilize, and inflation will increase mechanically as a result.	13	1
201512	1231	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	That’s my forecast, but I will admit that that analysis leaves me with some degree of unease.	13	1
201512	1232	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	In addition, although inflation expectations are broadly stable, they do appear to be under some downward pressure, and this is a challenge that the Committee simply has to meet.	13	1
201512	1233	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The fact that we’re much more likely than anyone would like to return to the zero lower bound makes this all the more important.	13	1
201512	1234	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Market participants should look back on this period and be convinced that the Committee is determined to get back to 2 percent inflation and is willing to run some risk of overshooting on resource utilization to do that, and only our behavior can carry that burden.	13	1
201512	1235	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	With that background, I have shifted to a slightly lower path for the policy rate in this SEP.	13	1
201512	1236	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I assume liftoff at this meeting and a total of three increases of 25 basis points each for 2016 and three more in 2017.	13	1
201512	1237	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I believe that for the next couple of years, economic weakness around the world will mean that the Committee can and should remove accommodation only gradually.	13	1
201512	1238	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I would conclude by commenting briefly on recent conditions in leveraged finance markets, which have been a topic today and for the past couple of weeks.	13	1
201512	1239	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	These markets have been in a correction since about midyear, particularly for the most risky assets, such as those rated triple-C or lower or not rated at all, and particularly among energy firms.	13	1
201512	1240	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	The Third Avenue announcement last week that the firm would refuse to allow withdrawals has put significant further pressure on these markets, and while the situation warrants careful monitoring, it’s worth saying that sharp changes in sentiment and liquidity are not at all unusual in leveraged finance markets.	13	1
201512	1241	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	I can’t say—no one can really say—whether this will portend a sustained turn in the credit cycle, but for now I don’t see this correction as having important implications for the real economy or for monetary policy.	13	1
201512	1242	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	In fact, given that rates are likely to stay low for several more years, there remain powerful incentives to reach for yield, and many professionals are saying that this is the best and, in fact, the first real buying opportunity in several years.	13	1
201512	1243	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	This isn’t a forecast, but it would not surprise me if, after all of this settles out, the leveraged finance markets return to a boil in the first half of 2016 after this correction.	13	1
201512	1244	2015-12-16	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201512	1247	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201512	1248	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	The Ninth District economy is expanding, but growth is modest and uneven.	13	1
201512	1249	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	There’s little evidence of rising inflation.	13	1
201512	1250	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	The strong dollar and slower growth in foreign economies are contributing to weaknesses in the District’s industrial, agriculture, energy, and mining sectors.	13	1
201512	1251	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	At the same time, urban areas are generally continuing to grow.	13	1
201512	1252	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Unemployment in the Twin Cities area fell to 2.9 percent in October, the lowest rate among metropolitan areas of 1 million or more.	13	1
201512	1253	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Notwithstanding this low unemployment, the BLS reports that the core CPI for Minneapolis–St Paul increased only 1.1 percent from the first half of 2014 to the first half of 2015.	13	1
201512	1254	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	The same combination of modest growth and low inflation prevails nationally and bears on the Committee’s deliberations.	13	1
201512	1255	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	In its October statement, the Committee specified two conditions for raising rates at this meeting:  further improvement in the labor markets and reasonable confidence that inflation would return to 2 percent over the medium term.	13	1
201512	1256	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	I think there’s no doubt that the US labor market has improved since October across a range of metrics, so I’ll focus my remaining remarks on the outlook for inflation.	13	1
201512	1257	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Core PCE inflation has been quite stable over the past year, running close to 1.3 percent.	13	1
201512	1258	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Although today’s CPI report shows some improvement, the CPI data still do not appear strong enough to bring the PCE inflation rate back to 2 percent.	13	1
201512	1259	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	The fact that core inflation is running consistently below target is important in light of the research literature on forecasting inflation.	13	1
201512	1260	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	This literature has repeatedly shown that it’s hard to improve on a forecast that equates future headline inflation to the recent trend value for core inflation.	13	1
201512	1261	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	As in October, such a forecast predicts that headline inflation will run below our 2 percent target.	13	1
201512	1262	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	In particular, the literature shows that adding the unemployment rate or other measures of economic activity alongside lags of core inflation does not notably improve forecast accuracy.	13	1
201512	1263	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Thus, we should not be very confident that continuing improvements in labor markets will automatically push up inflation.	13	1
201512	1264	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	It is true that headline inflation should rise toward core inflation as recent transitory declines in energy prices fade, but energy prices will likely play a minor role in the evolution of core inflation.	13	1
201512	1265	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Stabilization in the foreign exchange value of the dollar will help to stabilize import prices, which in turn will contribute to a modest uptick in core inflation.	13	1
201512	1266	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	But with disinflationary trends in place in most of the major exporting nations and currency values notoriously unpredictable, we shouldn’t count on import prices to restore US inflation to target.	13	1
201512	1267	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	What about other ways to gauge the likely direction of future inflation?	13	1
201512	1268	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Unfortunately, as has been the case for some time, neither market-based nor survey-based measures of inflation expectations instill much confidence that inflation is returning to our 2 percent target over the medium term.	13	1
201512	1269	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Market-based measures of inflation compensation remain near their historic lows.	13	1
201512	1270	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Survey-based measures have edged downward and are also near historic lows.	13	1
201512	1271	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	In sum, I see little reason to be more confident now than in October that inflation will return to 2 percent in the medium term.	13	1
201512	1272	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	In the next round, I’ll discuss how this may raise communication challenges for the Committee.	13	1
201512	1273	2015-12-16	meeting	LYON	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201512	1274	2015-12-16	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201512	1275	2015-12-16	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	13	1
201512	1276	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201512	1277	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	My baseline expectations have changed very little since October.	13	1
201512	1278	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The economy is chugging along at a moderately above-trend pace of growth.	13	1
201512	1279	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It’s revealed enough momentum to surmount the hurdles of the stronger dollar and sluggish global economy, at least up to this point, though it doesn’t seem to me that the upside risk would take us more than a couple of tenths of a percentage point higher than what people project.	13	1
201512	1280	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Now, when I was in the corridor at the coffee break, I heard a rumor that someone tomorrow is going to propose that we increase the federal funds rate.	13	1
201512	1281	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	[Laughter]	13	1
201512	1282	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	I was talking to you during that period, President Williams.	13	1
201512	1283	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	I wanted to address the formulation that we’ve had in our FOMC statements for some time with respect to improvements in the labor market and reasonable confidence that inflation would return to the target rate.	13	1
201512	1284	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Beginning with the labor market, we’ve seen a few signs that wage increases may finally be picking up, though judging how much of this is attributable to labor market tightening may be a bit difficult in light of the increases in state and local minimum wages that are going into effect in 2015 and 2016, as well as some increases by some large, low-wage employers that have been prompted by political and social considerations.	13	1
201512	1285	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	All of this should serve as a reminder that monthly wage numbers are pretty noisy.	13	1
201512	1286	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	On the issue of remaining slack, I think it’s still hard to say anything with great assurance on how much slack remains, and Jay has already well addressed this point.	13	1
201512	1287	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Apart from the continuing questions regarding wages, we have uncertainties about labor force participation and, more generally, the future behavior of those who are currently out of the labor market.	13	1
201512	1288	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Some have argued that nonparticipants are not a good indication of remaining slack, citing, among other things, recent declines in reentry rates from marginally attached workers and those outside the labor market.	13	1
201512	1289	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	But we’ve got to remember that, in many respects, we still have some quite unusual characteristics in the labor market relative to pre-crisis conditions.	13	1
201512	1290	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	On this point of reductions in the reentry rate of those out of the labor force, for example, it’s worth noting that the percentage of those who leave the labor force but continue to want a job is still well above levels that prevailed pretty steadily for a decade before the crisis.	13	1
201512	1291	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	And as it’s still the case that nearly half of those who exit the labor force from long-term unemployment will reenter within a year, this persistently large group of those leaving may—and I emphasize “may”—reflect some additional slack.	13	1
201512	1292	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	An additional point, just to reiterate, is that paying too much attention to a couple of months of data can be misleading.	13	1
201512	1293	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	For example, the recent decline in the percentage of the long- term unemployed finding a job followed an earlier increase, and that recent decline has basically taken us to where we were a couple of years ago.	13	1
201512	1294	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	These are yet more examples showing why I think it’s hard to draw too many conclusions from one data series about the labor market.	13	1
201512	1295	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	We still don’t know how much of what’s going on reflects secular changes from what we’ve seen before and how much will snap back to something more like pre-crisis conditions.	13	1
201512	1296	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	So has there been improvement in the labor market?	13	1
201512	1297	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	In a literal sense, of course there has been.	13	1
201512	1298	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	There’s been ongoing improvement for some time now.	13	1
201512	1299	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The relevant question, to me at least, is whether the improvement that has taken place is enough to justify raising the federal funds target range.	13	1
201512	1300	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	I don’t think it’s consistent with our statutory mandate to treat this question as a self-contained one that is unrelated to the issue of inflation.	13	1
201512	1301	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	After all, our statutory mandate is to promote maximum employment, not to promote improved employment conditions.	13	1
201512	1302	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	So the limit on our efforts to promote further employment in the labor market comes from the other part of the dual mandate, that of price stability.	13	1
201512	1303	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The formulation we adopted some time ago that it would be appropriate to lift off when labor market conditions had improved and there was reasonable assurance that inflation would return to the 2 percent objective was, I believe, a logical way of indicating that we had been falling short on both sides of the mandate in the same direction.	13	1
201512	1304	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	There’s no justification for removing accommodation that helps promote employment unless that stance would conflict with the price-stability side of the mandate or potentially pose a risk to financial stability that might lead to some very negative outcomes for both mandates.	13	1
201512	1305	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	What about inflation?	13	1
201512	1306	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Can we have reasonable assurance that, under current circumstances, inflation will return to 2 percent in the medium term, given that both headline and core have been running below that target for nearly the whole period since the financial crisis, and that this Committee has pretty consistently overestimated what inflation will be a year in advance?	13	1
201512	1307	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	As I understand it, the basic argument of those who have this assurance is that inflation is currently being held below that level essentially because of the decline in energy prices and the strengthening of the dollar, both of which are presumed to be transitory.	13	1
201512	1308	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	With the output gap presumed to be small and diminishing, a Phillips-curve relationship will then reassert itself.	13	1
201512	1309	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	That seems to me a plausible hypothesis, but, to be honest, it doesn’t seem more than that.	13	1
201512	1310	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	There are several reasons of quite different types why the hypothesis may not pan out as anticipated.	13	1
201512	1311	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	First, even if one stipulates the basic argument, it might be premature to make it under current circumstances.	13	1
201512	1312	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	And, as Governor Brainard pointed out, as one listened to views around the table today, this seems a somewhat unusual moment to be asserting reasonable confidence, a moment when people are marking down expectations for inflation and the downside risks associated with it.	13	1
201512	1313	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The recent additional slide in oil prices and expectations of the longer-term effect of a strong dollar may make the downward pressure on inflation last longer than had been expected, and some people began making this argument earlier this year.	13	1
201512	1314	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Alternatively, the output gap may be modestly to moderately larger than assumed.	13	1
201512	1315	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	This possibility returns us to the discussions we’ve been having more or less every meeting for several years now, to which I alluded a moment ago.	13	1
201512	1316	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	A second reason to doubt the hypothesis cuts more deeply into the mechanics of the theory that lies beneath it.	13	1
201512	1317	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Here there are two salient points, both of which I and others have made numerous times before.	13	1
201512	1318	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	One is the familiar observation that the Phillips-curve reasoning has not fared very well empirically for some years now.	13	1
201512	1319	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	While nearly everyone shares the intuition that, directionally at least, a narrowing output gap should lead to more inflation, there just hasn’t been very much observed data to back up that intuition for at least a decade, certainly not with anything like the precision that one would hope to find in a theory underlying important policy decisions.	13	1
201512	1320	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The second point is that the assumption of anchored expectations that plays an important role in the hypothesis is at least a little questionable.	13	1
201512	1321	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	For one thing, both market and survey measures of inflation expectations are at the low ends of their historical ranges; while it may be that liquidity considerations and gas prices, respectively, help explain these current measures, their very levels remind us that there’s no convincing theory—much less an empirically supported theory—of how expectations are formed, how in concrete terms anchored expectations function in the economy to affect inflation, and when expectations may become unanchored.	13	1
201512	1322	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It has been remarkable to me to observe how much weight is placed on a concept that has worked reasonably well for projecting inflation in the recent past but for which neither theory nor practice provides much ground for determining when and how it will continue to work in the future.	13	1
201512	1323	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It seems at least worth asking whether the persistence of some developments that are characterized as shocks in the models we use may over time themselves affect expectations.	13	1
201512	1324	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Or maybe the mere persistence of below-stated-target inflation over a period of years can have this effect.	13	1
201512	1325	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	If Narayana Kocherlakota were still here today, he might add that the apparent acquiescence of the FOMC in that situation might further chip away at the anchor keeping expectations in place.	13	1
201512	1326	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	A third reason to question the hypothesis is even more fundamental—that is, the possibility that current models for understanding inflation are missing a different dynamic that has taken hold in the economy.	13	1
201512	1327	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Some academics have argued as much.	13	1
201512	1328	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It certainly is not farfetched to wonder whether a shift in the so-called commodity supercycle, the slowing of emerging market growth on a secular basis, changes in US labor markets, and other phenomena have worked a more basic change in the sources and path of inflation.	13	1
201512	1329	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Here, as in many other areas, the financial crisis and Great Recession may have amplified or accelerated changes already working their way through the economy.	13	1
201512	1330	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	There is no particular reason to believe that the understanding about inflation developed in the Volcker and post-Volcker periods will be any more lasting than that which prevailed in the pre-Volcker period.	13	1
201512	1331	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	The obvious candidate for a quite different explanation is secular stagnation.	13	1
201512	1332	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	But even short of a theory affecting the entire performance of the economy, something else may be going on, and it is precisely at times when received wisdom is breaking down that policy missteps look, in retrospect, most likely to occur.	13	1
201512	1333	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Now, I certainly don’t think there’s enough evidence of secular stagnation, or variants thereof, to justify substituting some new theoretical approach to how inflation develops.	13	1
201512	1334	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	But there is a possibility that a significant shift in how the economy operates—for example, that inflation may be significantly more influenced by secular than cyclical factors right now—has been under way.	13	1
201512	1335	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	This is just another reason why I’ve been suggesting that we should rely a little less on faith that old correlations will reassert themselves and a little more on incoming data that actually show they are working.	13	1
201512	1336	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	More generally, I don’t want to reject what I’ve termed the “plausible hypothesis” that seems to be motivating much of the Committee.	13	1
201512	1337	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Were we currently seeing core inflation moving up, perhaps along with documented wage acceleration that seems tied to that inflation, it might well be reasonable to have confidence that inflation was finally returning to the Committee’s stated target.	13	1
201512	1338	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	By the way, no one— certainly not me—has been arguing for waiting until inflation actually gets all the way to 2 percent to begin acting.	13	1
201512	1339	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It’s more a matter of wanting to see data that document the fact that the trend is in that direction.	13	1
201512	1340	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	So, in the end, for me this is a matter of assessing which outcomes are more likely and which risks are of greater concern.	13	1
201512	1341	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	There is surely some chance that if we were to wait, the hypothesis will prove correct, and we would have to move more quickly to raise rates in the future in order to contain inflationary pressures that threaten to get away from us.	13	1
201512	1342	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Though with real GDP growth running only modestly above its trend rate, this seems to me a fairly modest risk.	13	1
201512	1343	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	In an environment, though, of global disinflation, with spreads on many asset classes already widening, with decent but mixed economic indicators, with our own statistical models suggesting that the underlying trend of PCE inflation is 1.8 percent or a little lower, and with an asymmetric box of tools with which to respond to a slowdown as opposed to more rapid growth, I think it’s difficult, at least for me, to have reasonable confidence that inflation will return to 2 percent.	13	1
201512	1344	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	It’s not unreasonable to have that belief, but the case for it still seems to me a little soft.	13	1
201512	1345	2015-12-16	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201512	1346	2015-12-16	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1

201512	1361	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Labor markets in the Fourth District continue to make steady progress.	13	1

201512	1363	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	District job growth has been running between 1¼ and 1½ percent over the past three years, which is considerably stronger than it was over the previous expansion when the region actually lost jobs.	13	1
201512	1364	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The District’s unemployment rate has fallen to 4.7 percent, which is more than ½ percentage point lower than the minimum reached during the last expansion in 2007.	13	1
201512	1365	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Shortages of qualified workers continue to be an issue in the District.	13	1

201512	1367	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	So far in our region, acceleration in wages is seen mainly in higher-skilled jobs, but some of our District contacts report that they will be implementing broad-based wage increases of 3 to 4 percent next year and significantly higher increases for high-skilled workers.	13	1
201512	1368	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Turning to the national economy:  In response to the incoming data, I’ve made only minor adjustments to my outlook since the September SEP.	13	1
201512	1369	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	In my view, the fundamentals supporting the expansion remain favorable, including highly accommodative monetary policy, household balance sheets that have improved greatly since the recession, and sustained strengthening in labor markets.	13	1
201512	1370	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	While lower oil prices will continue to weigh on investment- and energy-related sectors, lower energy costs will help offset some of the effects of the stronger dollar on businesses and will also support stronger consumer spending.	13	1
201512	1371	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Fiscal policy will also make a modestly positive contribution.	13	1
201512	1372	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I continue to expect GDP growth to pick up to an above-trend pace of 2.7 percent next year before moving down over 2017 and 2018 to trend, which I estimate at about 2¼ percent.	13	1
201512	1373	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I view the current economy at or nearly at full employment from the standpoint of our monetary policy goal.	13	1
201512	1374	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The unemployment rate is now about ¼ percentage point below my estimate of the longer-run rate of 5¼ percent.	13	1
201512	1375	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The labor force participation rate is now only about 30 basis points below its longer-run trend rate as estimated by Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland and Board staff.	13	1
201512	1376	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The pace of economic growth I’m projecting is sufficient to deliver some further improvement in labor markets, with payroll growth exceeding the range of 75,000 to 120,000 per month that various models indicate as sufficient to put downward pressure on the unemployment rate.	13	1
201512	1377	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I see the unemployment rate falling further below my longer-run rate next year, before gradually returning to that level by the end of 2018.	13	1
201512	1378	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I view inflation expectations as reasonably well anchored despite some small movements in our measures.	13	1
201512	1379	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	As the expansion continues and as the effects of the earlier and more recent declines in energy prices, non-energy commodity prices, and the appreciation of the dollar wane, I expect inflation to increase gradually, returning to our 2 percent goal sometime in 2017.	13	1
201512	1380	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	President Rosengren may feel better to know that the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland staff also analyzed the relationship between core PCE inflation and core CPI inflation, but they found that it was very difficult to reach any general conclusion about whether the gap between the two is usually closed by core PCE inflation rising toward the core CPI inflation rate or vice versa.	13	1
201512	1381	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	There are, of course, risks to my forecast, but I see them as balanced and not overly large.	13	1
201512	1382	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	On the downside, the dollar could appreciate more than I’ve assumed, perhaps reflecting divergent paths for economic growth and monetary policy abroad from those in the United States.	13	1
201512	1383	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Another risk is that oil prices could continue to decline rather than stabilize.	13	1
201512	1384	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Both of these could dampen growth in the trade and industrial sectors more than I’ve built into my forecast.	13	1
201512	1385	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	On the upside, lower oil prices may buoy consumer spending more than I anticipate.	13	1
201512	1386	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	With respect to inflation risks, the effects of oil price declines and dollar appreciation may be less transient and inflation could stay lower for longer than I’m projecting.	13	1
201512	1387	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	This could weigh on inflation expectations, which could then feed back into actual inflation.	13	1
201512	1388	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	On the other hand, accommodative monetary policy combined with an improving economy may lead to more-than-anticipated upward pressure on inflation over the medium run.	13	1
201512	1389	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	In fact, analysis by the Federal Reserve Bank of Cleveland’s staff shows that over the past 15 years, historical forecast errors of several highly regarded inflation forecasting models have been skewed to the upside—that is, the models have underestimated actual inflation.	13	1
201512	1390	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The policy path that underlies my modal forecast has the federal funds rate moving up at this meeting and then gradually up over the forecast horizon, reaching my longer-run level of 3½ percent in 2018.	13	1
201512	1391	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Of course, if incoming economic information leads to a significant change in my outlook, I’ll adjust this policy path as appropriate.	13	1
201512	1392	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I view one of the benefits of starting on this path now is that it will allow us to recalibrate policy over time as some of the uncertainty surrounding the longer-run level of interest rates, the economy’s potential growth rate, and the longer-run unemployment rate are resolved.	13	1
201512	1393	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Having policy react to changes in the outlook as informed by changes in economic conditions is how I interpret our being “data dependent.”	13	1
201512	1394	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Our policy rate path may well have to deviate from what our current expected path is if the economy’s evolution turns out to be different from what we currently anticipate.	13	1
201512	1395	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	I don’t view this as problematic, nor do I think we should refrain from taking appropriate action based on the current outlook solely because there’s some chance the outlook will change in a way that necessitates having to subsequently reverse the action.	13	1
201512	1396	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	The economy is dynamic, and there are always risks associated with the forecast which may ultimately manifest themselves.	13	1
201512	1397	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Appropriate policy has to be systematically responsive to material changes in the outlook.	13	1
201512	1398	2015-12-16	meeting	MESTER	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
